   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading mw-first-heading">Car</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>

    <div id="contentSub2"></div>

     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">
       Motorized passenger road vehicle
      </div> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style>
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       For the 2006 Pixar film, see <a href="/wiki/Cars_(film)" title="Cars (film)">Cars (film)</a>. For the country, see <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">Central African Republic</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Car_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Car (disambiguation)">Car (disambiguation)</a>, <a href="/wiki/CARS_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="CARS (disambiguation)">CARS</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Automobile_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Automobile (disambiguation)">Automobile</a>.
      </div> 
      <p class="mw-empty-elt"> </p>

      <p>A <b>car</b> (or <b>automobile</b>) is a wheeled <a href="/wiki/Motor_vehicle" title="Motor vehicle">motor vehicle</a> used for <a href="/wiki/Transportation" class="mw-redirect" title="Transportation">transportation</a>. Most definitions of <i>cars</i> say that they run primarily on roads, seat one to eight people, have four <a href="/wiki/Wheels" class="mw-redirect" title="Wheels">wheels</a>, and mainly transport people rather than goods.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OEDmotrcar_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OEDmotrcar-3">[3]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Cars came into global use during the 20th century, and <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed economies</a> depend on them. The year 1886 is regarded as the birth year of the car when German inventor <a href="/wiki/Carl_Benz" title="Carl Benz">Carl Benz</a> patented his <a href="/wiki/Benz_Patent-Motorwagen" title="Benz Patent-Motorwagen">Benz Patent-Motorwagen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-stein_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stein-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Daimler8586_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daimler8586-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GarrisonEG_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GarrisonEG-5">[5]</a></sup> Cars became widely available in the early 20th century. One of the first cars accessible to the masses was the 1908 <a href="/wiki/Ford_Model_T" title="Ford Model T">Model T</a>, an American car manufactured by the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford Motor Company</a>. Cars were rapidly adopted in the US, where they replaced <a href="/wiki/Draft_animal" class="mw-redirect" title="Draft animal">animal-drawn</a> <a href="/wiki/Carriage" title="Carriage">carriages</a> and carts.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-6">[6]</a></sup> In Europe and other parts of the world, demand for automobiles did not increase until after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-6">[6]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Cars have controls for driving, parking, passenger comfort, and a variety of lights. Over the decades, additional features and controls have been added to vehicles, making them progressively more complex. These include rear-reversing cameras, <a href="/wiki/Automobile_air_conditioning" title="Automobile air conditioning">air conditioning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Automotive_navigation_system" title="Automotive navigation system">navigation systems</a>, and <a href="/wiki/In-car_entertainment" title="In-car entertainment">in-car entertainment</a>. Most cars in use in the early 2020s are propelled by an <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine</a>, fueled by the <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a> of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>. <a href="/wiki/Electric_car" title="Electric car">Electric cars</a>, which were invented early in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_automobile" title="History of the automobile">history of the car</a>, became commercially available in the 2000s and are predicted to cost less to buy than gasoline cars before 2025.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The transition from fossil fuels to electric cars features prominently in most <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation_scenarios" class="mw-redirect" title="Climate change mitigation scenarios">climate change mitigation scenarios</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Project_Drawdown" class="mw-redirect" title="Project Drawdown">Project Drawdown</a>'s 100 actionable solutions for climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>There are costs and benefits to car use. The costs to the individual include acquiring the vehicle, interest payments (if the car is financed), repairs and <a href="/wiki/Auto_maintenance" class="mw-redirect" title="Auto maintenance">maintenance</a>, fuel, <a href="/wiki/Depreciation" title="Depreciation">depreciation</a>, driving time, <a href="/wiki/Parking_fee" class="mw-redirect" title="Parking fee">parking fees</a>, taxes, and insurance.<sup id="cite_ref-racv_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-racv-11">[11]</a></sup> The costs to society include <a href="/wiki/Maintaining_road" class="mw-redirect" title="Maintaining road">maintaining roads</a>, <a href="/wiki/Land_use" title="Land use">land use</a>, <a href="/wiki/Road_congestion" class="mw-redirect" title="Road congestion">road congestion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a>, <a href="/wiki/Health_care" title="Health care">healthcare</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_recycling" title="Vehicle recycling">disposing of the vehicle at the end of its life</a>. <a href="/wiki/Traffic_collisions" class="mw-redirect" title="Traffic collisions">Traffic collisions</a> are the largest cause of injury-related deaths worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-who_stats_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-who_stats-12">[12]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Personal benefits include on-demand transportation, mobility, independence, and convenience.<sup id="cite_ref-setright_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-setright-13">[13]</a></sup> Societal benefits include economic benefits, such as job and wealth creation from the <a href="/wiki/Automotive_industry" title="Automotive industry">automotive industry</a>, transportation provision, societal well-being from leisure and travel opportunities, and revenue generation from <a href="/wiki/Category:Vehicle_taxes" title="Category:Vehicle taxes">taxes</a>. People's ability to move flexibly from place to place has far-reaching implications for the nature of societies.<sup id="cite_ref-parking_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parking-14">[14]</a></sup> There are around 1 billion cars in use worldwide. Car usage is increasing rapidly, especially in <a href="/wiki/Automotive_industry_in_China" title="Automotive industry in China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Automotive_industry_in_India" title="Automotive industry in India">India</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Newly_industrialized_country" title="Newly industrialized country">newly industrialized countries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-plunkettresearch.com_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plunkettresearch.com-15">[15]</a></sup> </p>

      </div> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> word <i>car</i> is believed to originate from <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a> <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/carrus#Latin" class="extiw" title="wikt:carrus">carrus</a></i></span>/<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/carrum#Latin" class="extiw" title="wikt:carrum">carrum</a></i></span> "wheeled vehicle" or (via <a href="/wiki/Old_Norman" title="Old Norman">Old North French</a>) <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> <i>carre</i> "two-wheeled cart," both of which in turn derive from <a href="/wiki/Gaulish_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaulish language">Gaulish</a> <i>karros</i> "<a href="/wiki/Chariot" title="Chariot">chariot</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Carrus_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carrus-17">[17]</a></sup> It originally referred to any wheeled <a href="/wiki/Horse-drawn_vehicle" title="Horse-drawn vehicle">horse-drawn vehicle</a>, such as a <a href="/wiki/Cart" title="Cart">cart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carriage" title="Carriage">carriage</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Wagon" title="Wagon">wagon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OEDcar_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OEDcar-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>"Motor car," attested from 1895, is the usual formal term in <a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">British English</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OEDmotrcar_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OEDmotrcar-3">[3]</a></sup> "Autocar," a variant likewise attested from 1895 and literally meaning "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/self-propelled" class="extiw" title="wikt:self-propelled">self-propelled</a> car," is now considered archaic.<sup id="cite_ref-OEDautocar_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OEDautocar-20">[20]</a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/Horseless_carriage" title="Horseless carriage">Horseless carriage</a>" is attested from 1895.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>"Automobile," a <a href="/wiki/Classical_compound" title="Classical compound">classical compound</a> derived from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> <i>autós</i> (αὐτός) "self" and <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a> <i>mobilis</i> "movable," entered English from <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> and was first adopted by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Automobile_Club" title="Royal Automobile Club">Automobile Club of Great Britain</a> in 1897.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> It fell out of favour in Britain and is now used chiefly in <a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">North America</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-OEDautomob_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OEDautomob-23">[23]</a></sup> where the abbreviated form "auto" commonly appears as an adjective in compound formations like "<a href="/wiki/Auto_industry" class="mw-redirect" title="Auto industry">auto industry</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Auto_mechanic" title="Auto mechanic">auto mechanic</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Both forms are still used in everyday <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> (<i>auto</i>/<i>automobiel</i>) and <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> (<i>Auto</i>/<i>Automobil</i>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2> 
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096">
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_automobile" title="History of the automobile">History of the automobile</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:SteamMachineOfVerbiestIn1678.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/SteamMachineOfVerbiestIn1678.jpg/220px-SteamMachineOfVerbiestIn1678.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/SteamMachineOfVerbiestIn1678.jpg/330px-SteamMachineOfVerbiestIn1678.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/SteamMachineOfVerbiestIn1678.jpg/440px-SteamMachineOfVerbiestIn1678.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="573"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:SteamMachineOfVerbiestIn1678.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Steam Machine Of Verbiest, In 1678. (<a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Verbiest" title="Ferdinand Verbiest">Ferdinand Verbiest</a>)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The first working steam-powered vehicle was designed—and quite possibly built—by <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Verbiest" title="Ferdinand Verbiest">Ferdinand Verbiest</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Flemish_people" title="Flemish people">Flemish</a> member of a <a href="/wiki/Jesuit_China_missions" title="Jesuit China missions">Jesuit mission in China</a> around 1672. It was a 65-centimetre (26&nbsp;in)-long scale-model toy for the <a href="/wiki/Kangxi_Emperor" title="Kangxi Emperor">Kangxi Emperor</a> that was unable to carry a driver or a passenger.<sup id="cite_ref-setright_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-setright-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CuriousExp_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CuriousExp-27">[27]</a></sup> It is not known with certainty if Verbiest's model was successfully built or run.<sup id="cite_ref-CuriousExp_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CuriousExp-27">[27]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:FardierdeCugnot20050111.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/FardierdeCugnot20050111.jpg/220px-FardierdeCugnot20050111.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="122" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/FardierdeCugnot20050111.jpg/330px-FardierdeCugnot20050111.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/FardierdeCugnot20050111.jpg/440px-FardierdeCugnot20050111.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1085" data-file-height="600"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:FardierdeCugnot20050111.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Cugnot's 1771 <i>fardier à vapeur</i>, as preserved at the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_des_Arts_et_M%C3%A9tiers" title="Musée des Arts et Métiers">Musée des Arts et Métiers</a>, Paris, France
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Nicolas-Joseph_Cugnot" title="Nicolas-Joseph Cugnot">Nicolas-Joseph Cugnot</a> is widely credited with building the first full-scale, self-propelled mechanical vehicle or car in about 1769; he created a steam-powered tricycle.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> He also constructed two steam tractors for the French Army, one of which is preserved in the <a href="/wiki/Conservatoire_National_des_Arts_et_M%C3%A9tiers" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservatoire National des Arts et Métiers">French National Conservatory of Arts and Crafts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica-29">[29]</a></sup> His inventions were, however, handicapped by problems with water supply and maintaining steam pressure.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica-29">[29]</a></sup> In 1801, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Trevithick" title="Richard Trevithick">Richard Trevithick</a> built and demonstrated his <a href="/wiki/The_Puffing_Devil" class="mw-redirect" title="The Puffing Devil">Puffing Devil</a> road locomotive, believed by many to be the first demonstration of a steam-powered road vehicle. It was unable to maintain sufficient steam pressure for long periods and was of little practical use. </p>
      <p>The development of external combustion engines is detailed as part of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_car" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the car">history of the car</a> but often treated separately from the development of true cars. A variety of steam-powered road vehicles were used during the first part of the 19th century, including <a href="/wiki/Steam_car" title="Steam car">steam cars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steam_bus" title="Steam bus">steam buses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phaeton_(carriage)" title="Phaeton (carriage)">phaetons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Steam_roller" class="mw-redirect" title="Steam roller">steam rollers</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, sentiment against them led to the <a href="/wiki/Locomotive_Acts" title="Locomotive Acts">Locomotive Acts</a> of 1865. </p>
      <p>In 1807, <a href="/wiki/Nic%C3%A9phore_Ni%C3%A9pce" title="Nicéphore Niépce">Nicéphore Niépce</a> and his brother Claude created what was probably the world's first <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine</a> (which they called a <a href="/wiki/Pyr%C3%A9olophore" title="Pyréolophore">Pyréolophore</a>), but they chose to install it in a boat on the river <a href="/wiki/Saone" class="mw-redirect" title="Saone">Saone</a> in France.<sup id="cite_ref-NieMus_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NieMus-30">[30]</a></sup> Coincidentally, in 1807 the Swiss inventor <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Isaac_de_Rivaz" title="François Isaac de Rivaz">François Isaac de Rivaz</a> designed his own '<a href="/wiki/De_Rivaz_engine" title="De Rivaz engine">de Rivaz internal combustion engine</a>' and used it to develop the world's first vehicle to be powered by such an engine. The Niépces' Pyréolophore was fuelled by a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Lycopodium_powder" title="Lycopodium powder">Lycopodium powder</a> (dried spores of the <a href="/wiki/Lycopodium" title="Lycopodium">Lycopodium</a> plant), finely crushed coal dust and resin that were mixed with oil, whereas de Rivaz used a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NieMus_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NieMus-30">[30]</a></sup> Neither design was very successful, as was the case with others, such as <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Brown_(engineer)" title="Samuel Brown (engineer)">Samuel Brown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Morey" title="Samuel Morey">Samuel Morey</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Etienne_Lenoir" class="mw-redirect" title="Etienne Lenoir">Etienne Lenoir</a> with his <a href="/wiki/Hippomobile_(car)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hippomobile (car)">hippomobile</a>, who each produced vehicles (usually adapted carriages or carts) powered by internal combustion engines.<sup id="cite_ref-stein_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stein-1">[1]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Capture_d%E2%80%99%C3%A9cran_2016-10-14_%C3%A0_21.26.28.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Capture_d%E2%80%99%C3%A9cran_2016-10-14_%C3%A0_21.26.28.png/220px-Capture_d%E2%80%99%C3%A9cran_2016-10-14_%C3%A0_21.26.28.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Capture_d%E2%80%99%C3%A9cran_2016-10-14_%C3%A0_21.26.28.png/330px-Capture_d%E2%80%99%C3%A9cran_2016-10-14_%C3%A0_21.26.28.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Capture_d%E2%80%99%C3%A9cran_2016-10-14_%C3%A0_21.26.28.png/440px-Capture_d%E2%80%99%C3%A9cran_2016-10-14_%C3%A0_21.26.28.png 2x" data-file-width="791" data-file-height="577"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Capture_d%E2%80%99%C3%A9cran_2016-10-14_%C3%A0_21.26.28.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Gustave_Trouv%C3%A9" title="Gustave Trouvé">Gustave Trouvé</a>'s tricycle, the first ever electric automobile to be shown in public
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Carl-Benz_coloriert.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Carl-Benz_coloriert.jpg/170px-Carl-Benz_coloriert.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Carl-Benz_coloriert.jpg/255px-Carl-Benz_coloriert.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Carl-Benz_coloriert.jpg/340px-Carl-Benz_coloriert.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="1365"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Carl-Benz_coloriert.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Carl_Benz" title="Carl Benz">Carl Benz</a>, the inventor of the modern car
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In November 1881, French inventor <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Trouv%C3%A9" title="Gustave Trouvé">Gustave Trouvé</a> demonstrated the first working (three-wheeled) car powered by electricity at the <a href="/wiki/International_Exposition_of_Electricity,_Paris" class="mw-redirect" title="International Exposition of Electricity, Paris">International Exposition of Electricity, Paris</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Although several other German engineers (including <a href="/wiki/Gottlieb_Daimler" title="Gottlieb Daimler">Gottlieb Daimler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Maybach" title="Wilhelm Maybach">Wilhelm Maybach</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Siegfried_Marcus" title="Siegfried Marcus">Siegfried Marcus</a>) were working on the problem at about the same time. The year 1886 is regarded as the birth year of the car when the German <a href="/wiki/Carl_Benz" title="Carl Benz">Carl Benz</a> patented his <a href="/wiki/Benz_Patent-Motorwagen" title="Benz Patent-Motorwagen">Benz Patent-Motorwagen</a>; he is generally acknowledged as the inventor of the car.<sup id="cite_ref-stein_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stein-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Daimler8586_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daimler8586-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GarrisonEG_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GarrisonEG-5">[5]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:1885Benz.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/1885Benz.jpg/220px-1885Benz.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/1885Benz.jpg/330px-1885Benz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/1885Benz.jpg/440px-1885Benz.jpg 2x" data-file-width="976" data-file-height="768"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:1885Benz.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The original <a href="/wiki/Benz_Patent-Motorwagen" title="Benz Patent-Motorwagen">Benz Patent-Motorwagen</a>, first built in 1885 and awarded the patent for the concept
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1879, Benz was granted a patent for his first engine, which had been designed in 1878. Many of his other inventions made the use of the internal combustion engine feasible for powering a vehicle. His first <i>Motorwagen</i> was built in 1885 in <a href="/wiki/Mannheim" title="Mannheim">Mannheim</a>, Germany. He was awarded the patent for its invention as of his application on 29 January 1886 (under the auspices of his major company, <a href="/wiki/Benz_%26_Cie." class="mw-redirect" title="Benz &amp; Cie.">Benz &amp; Cie.</a>, which was founded in 1883). Benz began promotion of the vehicle on 3 July 1886, and about 25 Benz vehicles were sold between 1888 and 1893, when his first four-wheeler was introduced along with a cheaper model. They also were powered with <a href="/wiki/Four-stroke_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Four-stroke cycle">four-stroke</a> engines of his own design. Emile Roger of France, already producing Benz engines under license, now added the Benz car to his line of products. Because France was more open to the early cars, initially more were built and sold in France through Roger than Benz sold in Germany. In August 1888 <a href="/wiki/Bertha_Benz" title="Bertha Benz">Bertha Benz</a>, the wife of Carl Benz, undertook the first <a href="/wiki/Road_trip" title="Road trip">road trip</a> by car, to prove the road-worthiness of her husband's invention. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Berthabenzportrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Berthabenzportrait.jpg/170px-Berthabenzportrait.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Berthabenzportrait.jpg/255px-Berthabenzportrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Berthabenzportrait.jpg/340px-Berthabenzportrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3655" data-file-height="4867"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Berthabenzportrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Bertha_Benz" title="Bertha Benz">Bertha Benz</a>, the first long distance driver
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1896, Benz designed and patented the first internal-combustion <a href="/wiki/Flat_engine" title="Flat engine">flat engine</a>, called <i>boxermotor</i>. During the last years of the nineteenth century, Benz was the largest car company in the world with 572 units produced in 1899 and, because of its size, Benz &amp; Cie., became a <a href="/wiki/Joint-stock_company" title="Joint-stock company">joint-stock company</a>. The first motor car in central Europe and one of the first factory-made cars in the world, was produced by <a href="/wiki/Czech_lands" title="Czech lands">Czech</a> company Nesselsdorfer Wagenbau (later renamed to <a href="/wiki/Tatra_(company)" title="Tatra (company)">Tatra</a>) in 1897, the <a href="/wiki/Pr%C3%A4sident" title="Präsident">Präsident</a> automobil. </p>
      <p>Daimler and Maybach founded <a href="/wiki/Daimler_Motoren_Gesellschaft" title="Daimler Motoren Gesellschaft">Daimler Motoren Gesellschaft</a> (DMG) in <a href="/wiki/Cannstatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Cannstatt">Cannstatt</a> in 1890, and sold their first car in 1892 under the brand name <i>Daimler</i>. It was a horse-drawn stagecoach built by another manufacturer, which they retrofitted with an engine of their design. By 1895 about 30 vehicles had been built by Daimler and Maybach, either at the Daimler works or in the Hotel Hermann, where they set up shop after disputes with their backers. Benz, Maybach and the Daimler team seem to have been unaware of each other's early work. They never worked together; by the time of the merger of the two companies, Daimler and Maybach were no longer part of DMG. Daimler died in 1900 and later that year, Maybach designed an engine named <i>Daimler-Mercedes</i> that was placed in a specially ordered model built to specifications set by <a href="/wiki/Emil_Jellinek" title="Emil Jellinek">Emil Jellinek</a>. This was a production of a small number of vehicles for Jellinek to race and market in his country. Two years later, in 1902, a new model DMG car was produced and the model was named Mercedes after the Maybach engine, which generated 35&nbsp;hp. Maybach quit DMG shortly thereafter and opened a business of his own. Rights to the <i>Daimler</i> brand name were sold to other manufacturers. </p>
      <p>Carl Benz proposed co-operation between DMG and Benz &amp; Cie. when economic conditions began to deteriorate in Germany following the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a>, but the directors of DMG refused to consider it initially. Negotiations between the two companies resumed several years later when these conditions worsened, and in 1924, they signed an Agreement of Mutual Interest, valid until the year 2000. Both enterprises standardized design, production, purchasing, and sales and they advertised or marketed their car models jointly, although keeping their respective brands. On 28 June 1926, Benz &amp; Cie. and DMG finally merged as the Daimler-Benz company, baptizing all of its cars Mercedes Benz, as a brand honoring the most important model of the DMG cars, the Maybach design later referred to as the 1902 Mercedes-35&nbsp;hp, along with the Benz name. Carl Benz remained a member of the board of directors of Daimler-Benz until his death in 1929, and at times, his two sons also participated in the management of the company. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:%C3%89mile_Levassor.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/%C3%89mile_Levassor.jpg/170px-%C3%89mile_Levassor.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c0/%C3%89mile_Levassor.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="229" data-file-height="278"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:%C3%89mile_Levassor.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Émile Levassor
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Armand_Peugeot.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Armand_Peugeot.jpg/170px-Armand_Peugeot.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Armand_Peugeot.jpg/255px-Armand_Peugeot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Armand_Peugeot.jpg/340px-Armand_Peugeot.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1004" data-file-height="1330"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Armand_Peugeot.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Armand Peugeot
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1890, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Levassor" title="Émile Levassor">Émile Levassor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armand_Peugeot" title="Armand Peugeot">Armand Peugeot</a> of <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> began producing vehicles with Daimler engines, and so laid the foundation of the <a href="/wiki/Automotive_industry_in_France#1900_to_1950" title="Automotive industry in France">automotive industry in France</a>. In 1891, <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Doriot" title="Auguste Doriot">Auguste Doriot</a> and his Peugeot colleague Louis Rigoulot completed the longest trip by a gasoline-powered vehicle when their self-designed and built Daimler powered <a href="/wiki/Peugeot_Type_3" title="Peugeot Type 3">Peugeot Type 3</a> completed 2,100&nbsp;km (1,300 miles) from <a href="/wiki/Valentigney" title="Valentigney">Valentigney</a> to Paris and Brest and back again. They were attached to the first <a href="/wiki/Paris%E2%80%93Brest%E2%80%93Paris" title="Paris–Brest–Paris">Paris–Brest–Paris</a> bicycle race, but finished 6 days after the winning cyclist, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Terront" title="Charles Terront">Charles Terront</a>. </p>
      <p>The first design for an American car with a gasoline internal combustion engine was made in 1877 by <a href="/wiki/George_Baldwin_Selden" class="mw-redirect" title="George Baldwin Selden">George Selden</a> of <a href="/wiki/Rochester,_New_York" title="Rochester, New York">Rochester, New York</a>. Selden applied for a patent for a car in 1879, but the patent application expired because the vehicle was never built. After a delay of sixteen years and a series of attachments to his application, on 5 November 1895, Selden was granted a United States patent (<span><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://patents.google.com/patent/US549160">U.S. Patent 549,160</a></span>) for a <a href="/wiki/Two-stroke_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Two-stroke cycle">two-stroke</a> car engine, <a href="/wiki/George_B._Selden#The_Selden_patent" title="George B. Selden">which hindered, more than encouraged</a>, development of cars in the United States. His patent was challenged by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford" title="Henry Ford">Henry Ford</a> and others, and overturned in 1911. </p>
      <p>In 1893, the first running, gasoline-powered <a href="/wiki/Automotive_industry_in_the_United_States" title="Automotive industry in the United States">American car</a> was built and road-tested by the <a href="/wiki/Duryea_brothers" class="mw-redirect" title="Duryea brothers">Duryea brothers</a> of <a href="/wiki/Springfield,_Massachusetts" title="Springfield, Massachusetts">Springfield, Massachusetts</a>. The first public run of the <a href="/wiki/Duryea_Motor_Wagon" title="Duryea Motor Wagon">Duryea Motor Wagon</a> took place on 21 September 1893, on Taylor Street in <a href="/wiki/Metro_Center,_Springfield,_Massachusetts" title="Metro Center, Springfield, Massachusetts">Metro Center</a> Springfield.<sup id="cite_ref-ausbcomp.com_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ausbcomp.com-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Studebaker" title="Studebaker">The Studebaker Automobile Company</a>, subsidiary of a long-established wagon and coach manufacturer, started to build cars in 1897<sup id="cite_ref-Longst_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Longst-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: p.66">: p.66 </span></sup> and commenced sales of electric vehicles in 1902 and gasoline vehicles in 1904.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In Britain, there had been several attempts to build steam cars with varying degrees of success, with <a href="/wiki/Rickett_(car)" title="Rickett (car)">Thomas Rickett</a> even attempting a production run in 1860.<sup id="cite_ref-V&amp;VCars_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-V&amp;VCars-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Santler_(car)" class="mw-redirect" title="Santler (car)">Santler</a> from Malvern is recognized by the Veteran Car Club of Great Britain as having made the first gasoline-powered car in the country in 1894,<sup id="cite_ref-Beaulieu_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beaulieu-37">[37]</a></sup> followed by <a href="/wiki/Frederick_William_Lanchester" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick William Lanchester">Frederick William Lanchester</a> in 1895, but these were both one-offs.<sup id="cite_ref-Beaulieu_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beaulieu-37">[37]</a></sup> The first production vehicles in Great Britain came from the <a href="/wiki/Daimler_Company" title="Daimler Company">Daimler Company</a>, a company founded by <a href="/wiki/Harry_J._Lawson" class="mw-redirect" title="Harry J. Lawson">Harry J. Lawson</a> in 1896, after purchasing the right to use the name of the engines. Lawson's company made its first car in 1897, and they bore the name <i>Daimler</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Beaulieu_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beaulieu-37">[37]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1892, German engineer <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Diesel" title="Rudolf Diesel">Rudolf Diesel</a> was granted a patent for a "New Rational Combustion Engine". In 1897, he built the first <a href="/wiki/Diesel_engine" title="Diesel engine">diesel engine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-stein_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stein-1">[1]</a></sup> Steam-, electric-, and gasoline-powered vehicles competed for decades, with gasoline internal combustion engines achieving dominance in the 1910s. Although various <a href="/wiki/Pistonless_rotary_engine" title="Pistonless rotary engine">pistonless rotary engine</a> designs have attempted to compete with the conventional <a href="/wiki/Piston" title="Piston">piston</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crankshaft" title="Crankshaft">crankshaft</a> design, only <a href="/wiki/Mazda_Wankel_engine" title="Mazda Wankel engine">Mazda</a>'s version of the <a href="/wiki/Wankel_engine" title="Wankel engine">Wankel engine</a> has had more than very limited success. </p>
      <p>All in all, it is estimated that over 100,000 patents created the modern automobile and motorcycle.<sup id="cite_ref-TICCIHturin_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TICCIHturin-38">[38]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mass_production">Mass production</span></h2> 
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096">
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Automotive_industry" title="Automotive industry">Automotive industry</a>

      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:2011_Toyota_Corolla_--_NHTSA.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/2011_Toyota_Corolla_--_NHTSA.jpg/220px-2011_Toyota_Corolla_--_NHTSA.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/2011_Toyota_Corolla_--_NHTSA.jpg/330px-2011_Toyota_Corolla_--_NHTSA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/2011_Toyota_Corolla_--_NHTSA.jpg/440px-2011_Toyota_Corolla_--_NHTSA.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2652" data-file-height="1580"></a>

         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Corolla" title="Toyota Corolla">Toyota Corolla</a> is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_automobiles" title="List of best-selling automobiles">best-selling car of all-time</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Large-scale, <a href="/wiki/Production-line" class="mw-redirect" title="Production-line">production-line</a> manufacturing of affordable cars was started by <a href="/wiki/Ransom_Olds" class="mw-redirect" title="Ransom Olds">Ransom Olds</a> in 1901 at his <a href="/wiki/Oldsmobile" title="Oldsmobile">Oldsmobile</a> factory in <a href="/wiki/Lansing,_Michigan" title="Lansing, Michigan">Lansing, Michigan</a> and based upon stationary <a href="/wiki/Assembly_line" title="Assembly line">assembly line</a> techniques pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Marc_Isambard_Brunel" title="Marc Isambard Brunel">Marc Isambard Brunel</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth_Block_Mills" title="Portsmouth Block Mills">Portsmouth Block Mills</a>, England, in 1802. The assembly line style of mass production and interchangeable parts had been pioneered in the U.S. by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Blanchard_(inventor)" title="Thomas Blanchard (inventor)">Thomas Blanchard</a> in 1821, at the <a href="/wiki/Springfield_Armory" title="Springfield Armory">Springfield Armory</a> in <a href="/wiki/Springfield,_Massachusetts" title="Springfield, Massachusetts">Springfield, Massachusetts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> This concept was greatly expanded by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford" title="Henry Ford">Henry Ford</a>, beginning in 1913 with the world's first <i>moving</i> assembly line for cars at the <a href="/wiki/Highland_Park_Ford_Plant" title="Highland Park Ford Plant">Highland Park Ford Plant</a>. </p>
      <p>As a result, Ford's cars came off the line in fifteen-minute intervals, much faster than previous methods, increasing productivity eightfold, while using less manpower (from 12.5-man-hours to 1 hour 33 minutes).<sup id="cite_ref-Georgano_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Georgano-40">[40]</a></sup> It was so successful, <a href="/wiki/Paint" title="Paint">paint</a> became a bottleneck. Only <a href="/wiki/Japan_black" title="Japan black">Japan black</a> would dry fast enough, forcing the company to drop the variety of colors available before 1913, until fast-drying <a href="/wiki/Duco" title="Duco">Duco</a> <a href="/wiki/Lacquer" title="Lacquer">lacquer</a> was developed in 1926. This is the source of Ford's <a href="/wiki/Apocryphal" class="mw-redirect" title="Apocryphal">apocryphal</a> remark, "any color as long as it's black".<sup id="cite_ref-Georgano_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Georgano-40">[40]</a></sup> In 1914, an assembly line worker could buy a Model T with four months' pay.<sup id="cite_ref-Georgano_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Georgano-40">[40]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Ford's complex safety procedures—especially assigning each worker to a specific location instead of allowing them to roam about—dramatically reduced the rate of injury.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The combination of high wages and high efficiency is called "<a href="/wiki/Fordism" title="Fordism">Fordism</a>," and was copied by most major industries. The efficiency gains from the assembly line also coincided with the economic rise of the United States. The assembly line forced workers to work at a certain pace with very repetitive motions which led to more output per worker while other countries were using less productive methods. </p>
      <p>In the automotive industry, its success was dominating, and quickly spread worldwide seeing the founding of Ford France and Ford Britain in 1911, Ford Denmark 1923, Ford Germany 1925; in 1921, <a href="/wiki/Citro%C3%ABn" title="Citroën">Citroën</a> was the first native European manufacturer to adopt the production method. Soon, companies had to have assembly lines, or risk going broke; by 1930, 250 companies which did not, had disappeared.<sup id="cite_ref-Georgano_40-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Georgano-40">[40]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Development of automotive technology was rapid, due in part to the hundreds of small manufacturers competing to gain the world's attention. Key developments included electric <a href="/wiki/Ignition_system" title="Ignition system">ignition</a> and the electric self-starter (both by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Kettering" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Kettering">Charles Kettering</a>, for the <a href="/wiki/Cadillac" title="Cadillac">Cadillac</a> Motor Company in 1910–1911), independent <a href="/wiki/Suspension_(vehicle)" class="mw-redirect" title="Suspension (vehicle)">suspension</a>, and four-wheel brakes. </p>
      <p>Since the 1920s, nearly all cars have been mass-produced to meet market needs, so marketing plans often have heavily influenced car design. It was <a href="/wiki/Alfred_P._Sloan" title="Alfred P. Sloan">Alfred P. Sloan</a> who established the idea of different makes of cars produced by one company, called the <a href="/wiki/General_Motors_Companion_Make_Program" class="mw-redirect" title="General Motors Companion Make Program">General Motors Companion Make Program</a>, so that buyers could "move up" as their fortunes improved. </p>
      <p>Reflecting the rapid pace of change, makes shared parts with one another so larger production volume resulted in lower costs for each price range. For example, in the 1930s, <a href="/wiki/LaSalle_(automobile)" title="LaSalle (automobile)">LaSalles</a>, sold by <a href="/wiki/Cadillac" title="Cadillac">Cadillac</a>, used cheaper mechanical parts made by <a href="/wiki/Oldsmobile" title="Oldsmobile">Oldsmobile</a>; in the 1950s, <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet" title="Chevrolet">Chevrolet</a> shared hood, doors, roof, and windows with <a href="/wiki/Pontiac_(automobile)" title="Pontiac (automobile)">Pontiac</a>; by the 1990s, corporate <a href="/wiki/Powertrain" class="mw-redirect" title="Powertrain">powertrains</a> and shared <a href="/wiki/Automobile_platform" class="mw-redirect" title="Automobile platform">platforms</a> (with interchangeable <a href="/wiki/Brake" title="Brake">brakes</a>, suspension, and other parts) were common. Even so, only major makers could afford high costs, and even companies with decades of production, such as <a href="/wiki/Apperson" title="Apperson">Apperson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cole_Motor_Car_Company" title="Cole Motor Car Company">Cole</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dorris_Motors_Corporation" title="Dorris Motors Corporation">Dorris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haynes_Automobile_Company" title="Haynes Automobile Company">Haynes</a>, or Premier, could not manage: of some two hundred American car makers in existence in 1920, only 43 survived in 1930, and with the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>, by 1940, only 17 of those were left.<sup id="cite_ref-Georgano_40-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Georgano-40">[40]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In Europe, much the same would happen. <a href="/wiki/Morris_Motors" title="Morris Motors">Morris</a> set up its production line at <a href="/wiki/Cowley,_Oxford" class="mw-redirect" title="Cowley, Oxford">Cowley</a> in 1924, and soon outsold Ford, while beginning in 1923 to follow Ford's practice of <a href="/wiki/Vertical_integration" title="Vertical integration">vertical integration</a>, buying <a href="/wiki/Hotchkiss_(car)" title="Hotchkiss (car)">Hotchkiss</a> (engines), <a href="/wiki/EG_Wrigley_and_Company" title="EG Wrigley and Company">Wrigley</a> (gearboxes), and Osberton (radiators), for instance, as well as competitors, such as <a href="/wiki/Wolseley_Motors" title="Wolseley Motors">Wolseley</a>: in 1925, Morris had 41% of total British car production. Most British small-car assemblers, from <a href="/wiki/Abbey_(1922_automobile)" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbey (1922 automobile)">Abbey</a> to <a href="/wiki/Xtra_(automobile)" title="Xtra (automobile)">Xtra</a>, had gone under. Citroën did the same in France, coming to cars in 1919; between them and other cheap cars in reply such as <a href="/wiki/Renault" title="Renault">Renault</a>'s 10CV and <a href="/wiki/Peugeot" title="Peugeot">Peugeot</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Peugeot_5CV" title="Peugeot 5CV">5CV</a>, they produced 550,000 cars in 1925, and <a href="/wiki/Mors_(automobile)" title="Mors (automobile)">Mors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hurtu" title="Hurtu">Hurtu</a>, and others could not compete.<sup id="cite_ref-Georgano_40-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Georgano-40">[40]</a></sup> Germany's first mass-manufactured car, the <a href="/wiki/Opel" title="Opel">Opel</a> <a href="/wiki/Opel_Laubfrosch" title="Opel Laubfrosch">4PS <i>Laubfrosch</i></a> (Tree Frog), came off the line at <a href="/wiki/R%C3%BCsselsheim" class="mw-redirect" title="Rüsselsheim">Rüsselsheim</a> in 1924, soon making Opel the top car builder in Germany, with 37.5% of the market.<sup id="cite_ref-Georgano_40-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Georgano-40">[40]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In Japan, car production was very limited before World War II. Only a handful of companies were producing vehicles in limited numbers, and these were small, three-wheeled for commercial uses, like <a href="/wiki/Daihatsu" title="Daihatsu">Daihatsu</a>, or were the result of partnering with European companies, like <a href="/wiki/Isuzu_Motors" class="mw-redirect" title="Isuzu Motors">Isuzu</a> building the <a href="/wiki/Wolseley_Motors" title="Wolseley Motors">Wolseley A-9</a> in 1922. <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi" title="Mitsubishi">Mitsubishi</a> was also partnered with <a href="/wiki/Fiat_S.p.A." title="Fiat S.p.A.">Fiat</a> and built the <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_Model_A" title="Mitsubishi Model A">Mitsubishi Model A</a> based on a Fiat vehicle. <a href="/wiki/Toyota" title="Toyota">Toyota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nissan" title="Nissan">Nissan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suzuki" title="Suzuki">Suzuki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mazda" title="Mazda">Mazda</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Honda" title="Honda">Honda</a> began as companies producing non-automotive products before the war, switching to car production during the 1950s. Kiichiro Toyoda's decision to take <a href="/wiki/Toyoda_Loom_Works" class="mw-redirect" title="Toyoda Loom Works">Toyoda Loom Works</a> into automobile manufacturing would create what would eventually become <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Motor_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Toyota Motor Corporation">Toyota Motor Corporation</a>, the largest automobile manufacturer in the world. <a href="/wiki/Subaru" title="Subaru">Subaru</a>, meanwhile, was formed from a conglomerate of six companies who banded together as <a href="/wiki/Fuji_Heavy_Industries" class="mw-redirect" title="Fuji Heavy Industries">Fuji Heavy Industries</a>, as a result of having been broken up under <i><a href="/wiki/Keiretsu" title="Keiretsu">keiretsu</a></i> legislation. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_and_propulsion_technologies">Fuel and propulsion technologies</span></h2>
